> analysisThus remainder is 11069.
> 
> Now we should double-check the values for g(4M) and others, ensuring no mistakes.
> 
> We computed g(4M) = 8 with representation target d=4. Let's verify using our earlier algorithm: d=4, we found t=8 with K=1 (i.e., target = 12) and representation a=4, b=8.
> 
> Let's verify constraints: We need three distinct divisors of L = t * M = 8M such that they sum to N = M + 4M = 5M.
> 
> Our representation: we have divisor M (value 1*M). We also have divisor a' = a * (M/3^{K})? Wait we need to map a and b back to actual numbers.
> 
> We have K=1, target d*3^{K} = 12 = a + b with a=4,b=8.
> 
> We had earlier mapping: The actual divisors are a' = a * (M / 3^{K})? Let's derive.
> 
> Recall: We set N = M + c where c = d * M. We wrote condition:
> 
> We want numbers a', b' such that a'+b' = d * M.
> 
> We set K (the max exponent) = K (not to confuse with original K exponent of M). Let's denote k.
> 
> We derived that for any representation of d as sum of r1 + r2 where r_i = s_i / 3^{k_i}, we get actual numbers a' = s_i * 3^{K - k_i} (where K is the exponent of M). Since we factored M = 3^K, a' = r_i * M. Indeed a' = (s_i / 3^{k_i}) * M.
> 
> Thus if we have representation d = a_r + b_r where a_r = a / 3^{K} ?? Let's compute.
> 
> We have d * 3^{k} = a + b, with a = s1 * 3^{e1}, b = s2 * 3^{e2}. Then define k = max(e1,e2). Then we can express:
> 
> a' = a * 3^{K - k} = s1 * 3^{e1 + K - k} = s1 * 3^{K - (k - e1)}. Similarly, b' = s2 * 3^{K - (k - e2)}.
> 
> Thus a' and b' are multiples of M divided by 3^{k - e1} etc.
> 
> For our example: d=4, K=1 (k in earlier notation). We have a=4 = s1 * 3^{e1}. Since 4 = 4 * 3^0 => s1=4, e1=0. b=8 = 8 * 3^0 => s2=8, e2=0. So k = max(0,0) = 0. Actually we have k=0, not 1. Wait but we used target = d * 3^{K} where K is the exponent we multiply by 3 (the scaling). We set K=1, target = 4 * 3^1 = 12. So a=4, b=8 correspond to representation of 12. So k = max(e1,e2) = 0? But a=4 includes factor 3^0, b=8 also. So k=0.
> 
> Now we have N = M + c = M + 4M = 5M.
> 
> We need actual numbers: a' = a * (M / 3^{k}) = a * M (since k=0) = 4M, b' = 8M, but then sum a'+b' = 12M, not 4M. Wait we messed.
> 
> Hold on: The sum we need to represent is c = d * M = 4M. The representation of c = a' + b' where a', b' are divisors of L. Our scaling using target = d * 3^{K} maybe misapplied.
> 
> Let's derive correctly.
> 
> We need a', b' divisors of L = t * M, where a' = s1 * 3^{e1} * something? Actually any divisor of L can be expressed as s * 3^{e} where s|t, 0 ≤ e ≤ K (the exponent of M). Since L = t * 3^{K}, with K huge (2025!). So divisor a' = s1 * 3^{e1} where s1|t, e1 ≤ K.
> 
> Thus a' can be expressed as (s1 * 3^{e1}) * (3^{0})? Actually M = 3^{K}. So a' = s1 * 3^{e1}, with e1 ranging 0..K. The value of a' relative to M: a' = (s1 * 3^{e1}) = (s1 * 3^{e1 - K}) * M if e1≥K else not a multiple of M. Since K huge, e1 ≤ K, and for e1 < K, a' is smaller than M, not multiple of M. So to get a' of order M, we need e1 close to K.
> 
> Our earlier reduction assumed we take one divisor M itself (s=1,e=K). For other two divisors we allow e1, e2 maybe less than K, making them smaller than M.
> 
> We need a' + b' = c = 4M (since we already have M as first divisor). So we need a' + b' = 4M.
> 
> Thus a' and b' could be of size up to about 4M.
> 
> If we write a' = s1 * 3^{e1} with e1 close to K, say e1 = K - k1. Then a' = s1 * 3^{K - k1} = (s1 / 3^{k1}) * M.
> 
> Similarly b' = (s2 / 3^{k2}) * M.
> 
> Thus a'/M = s1 / 3^{k1}, b'/M = s2 / 3^{k2}. The sum (a' + b')/M = s1/3^{k1} + s2/3^{k2} = 4.
> 
> Thus we need representation of 4 as sum of rationals with denominators powers of 3 and numerators dividing t.
> 
> Thus we earlier defined d = 4. So our algorithm for d is correct.
> 
> Now we found representation using K (the scaling exponent) = 1? Actually we solved representation for d * 3^{K} = 12. But we should set target = d * 3^{k} where k is max(k1,k2). In our found solution: We took K (uppercase) = 1, leading to representation of 12 = 4 + 8, with denominators 3^{0}. So k1 = k2 = 0 (since a,b have no denominator), thus a'/M = 4, b'/M = 8, sum 12, not 4. Wait 4 +8 =12, not 4. So we need a'/M + b'/M = 4. Our representation gave 12. So we need to multiply by factor? Let's re-evaluate.
> 
> We took target = d * 3^{K} = 12, where K=1. The representation a=4, b=8 gave a/M' + b/M' = 4/3 + 8/3? Not right.
> 
> Let's compute a'/M from a, b, K.
> 
> We have a = 4 = s1 * 3^{e1}, b = 8 = s2 * 3^{e2}. k = max(e1,e2) = 0 (since both e1=e2=0). Then we have a'/M = s1 / 3^{k - e1}? Wait earlier formula: a' = a * 3^{K - k} where K is the exponent of M (large), and k is the max exponent among a,b. Since a and b have exponent e1/e2 relative to base 3 (not relative to M). In the absolute exponent scale, a = s1 * 3^{e1}, but the actual divisor a' = s1 * 3^{e1} * 3^{K - k}? Need to unify.
> 
> Let's think: In L = t * 3^{K_large}, where K_large = 2025!. Divisors have form d = s * 3^{e} with 0 ≤ e ≤ K_large, s|t.
> 
> Our a and b are such divisors. So we can write a = s1 * 3^{e1}, b = s2 * 3^{e2}.
> 
> Define k = max(e1, e2). Then a = s1 * 3^{e1} = (s1 / 3^{k-e1}) * 3^{k}. Since k-e1 could be negative? Actually if e1 <= k, then factor as s1 * 3^{e1} = (s1 * 3^{e1 - k}) * 3^{k}. But e1 - k is negative or zero, meaning fraction. Not convenient.
> 
> Better to express a/M = a / 3^{K_large} = s1 * 3^{e1 - K_large} = (s1 / 3^{K_large - e1}) = rational with denominator 3^{K_large - e1}.
> 
> Thus a'/M = s1 / 3^{K_large - e1}. Since K_large is huge, K_large - e1 is large. Therefore a'/M is extremely small unless s1 large.
> 
> Our earlier representation used a'/M = s1 / 3^{k1} where k1 = K_large - e1.
> 
> Thus k1, k2 are large numbers (the difference between K_large and the exponent e1,e2). But we can treat them as any non-negative integers (since e1 can range from 0 to K_large). Thus effectively we can choose arbitrary non-negative integers k1, k2 (the denominators). So representation of d = 4 as s1/3^{k1} + s2/3^{k2} with s1,s2 dividing t.
> 
> Thus our earlier algorithm should target d = 4 directly (not multiplied by extra powers). Indeed we need to find s1, s2, k1, k2 such that s1/3^{k1} + s2/3^{k2} = 4, with s1,s2|t, and we want minimal t.
> 
> Our min_t_for_d function attempted to find such by checking targets d * 3^{K} = a + b, but that seems to multiply both sides by 3^{K} where K = max(k1,k2). Indeed if we let K = max(k1,k2), then we have s1 * 3^{K - k1} + s2 * 3^{K - k2} = 4 * 3^{K}. Let a = s1 * 3^{K - k1}, b = s2 * 3^{K - k2}. So a + b = 4 * 3^{K}.
> 
> Thus our algorithm min_t_for_target_exclude(target, exclude_val) for target = d * 3^{K} is correct for each K.
> 
> Now we found minimal t=8 with K=1 (target=12) and representation a=4, b=8. Let's verify that this yields s1, s2, k1, k2.
> 
> Given K=1, we have a = 4 = s1 * 3^{K - k1}. So 4 = s1 * 3^{1 - k1}. Since 3^{1 - k1} must be integer, possible k1 = 0 => factor 3^{1}=3, then s1 = 4/3 not integer. Not allowed.
> 
> If k1 = 1 => exponent 3^{0}=1, s1 = 4. So k1=1, s1=4.
> 
> Similarly b = 8 = s2 * 3^{1 - k2}. If k2 = 1 => s2 = 8. So k2 =1, s2=8.
> 
> Thus we have k1 = k2 = 1. Then a/M = s1/3^{k1} = 4/3, b/M = 8/3, sum = 12/3 = 4, correct.
> 
> Thus s1=4, s2=8 divide t=8? Indeed t must be a multiple of both 4 and 8 => t at least 8. LCM(4,8)=8. So t=8 works.
> 
> Thus representation consistent.
> 
> Now need to ensure a',b' are distinct and not equal to M (which corresponds to s=1,k=0). Indeed s1=4,k1=1 gives a'/M = 4/3, which is not 1, so a' ≠ M. Good.
> 
> Thus g(4M) = t = 8.
> 
> Now for c=0 case we computed g(0) = 2/3, we need to ensure correct. Let's verify via representation earlier: N = M, we used divisors: M/3 (3^{K-1}), 2M/3 (2*3^{K-1}), M (3^{K}). t = 2? Let's compute.
> 
> We have L = 2 * M (t=2). Divisors: 3^{K-1}=M/3, 2*3^{K-1}=2M/3, 3^{K}=M.
> 
> LCM = 2M/3? Actually compute LCM of those three numbers: M/3 = 3^{K-1}, 2M/3 = 2*3^{K-1}, M = 3^{K}. LCM of 3^{K-1}, 2*3^{K-1}, 3^{K} = 2*3^{K} = 2M? Wait LCM of M/3 and M is M, but also 2M/3 shares factor 2, so LCM is 2M? Let's compute: M = 3^K. M/3 = 3^{K-1}. 2M/3 = 2 * 3^{K-1}. LCM of 3^{K-1} and 2*3^{K-1} is 2*3^{K-1}. LCM of that with M = 3^{K} (which is 3 * 3^{K-1}) is 2*3^{K} (since need both factor 2 and 3^K). So LCM = 2 * 3^{K} = 2M. That's indeed L = t * M with t=2. So f(M) = 2M? Wait earlier we got L = (2/3) M as minimal? Let's recompute.
> 
> Wait we had L = t * M, with t = minimal integer such that representation works. For c=0 case we have sum = M, we used three divisors: M/3, 2M/3, M. The LCM of these is 2M (since includes factor 2). So L = 2M, not (2/3)M.
> 
> But earlier we computed f(M) = (2/3)M based on L = (2/3)M (maybe miscalculated). Let's verify.
> 
> Our earlier representation for c=0: M = 3^{K}. We used a = 3^{K-2}, b = 2*3^{K-2}, c = M. Indeed a = M/9, b = 2M/9? Wait 3^{K-2}=3^{K}/9 = M/9. b = 2*3^{K-2}=2M/9.
> 
> Sum: M/9 + 2M/9 + M = (3M/9) + M = (M/3) + M = (4/3)M, not equal M. So earlier we made a mistake. Let's recompute carefully.
> 
> We need three distinct divisors of some integer L that sum to N = M (since c=0). The divisors do not need to include M necessarily. We attempted representation using powers of 3.
> 
> We need to find minimal L where there exist three distinct divisors summing to M.
> 
> Our earlier successful representation: we said a = 3^{K-2}, b = 2*3^{K-2}, c = M. Let's compute sum:
> 
> a = 3^{K-2} = M / 9
> 
> b = 2 * 3^{K-2} = 2M / 9
> 
> c = M
> 
> Sum = M + (M/9)*(1+2) = M + (3M/9) = M + M/3 = (4/3) M. Not equal M.
> 
> Thus that representation is invalid.
> 
> Our earlier computation that gave g(0)=2/3 probably wrong.
> 
> We must recompute g(0) correctly.
> 
> Let's solve the problem for c=0 directly: target N = M (odd power of 3). Need three distinct positive divisors of some L (which must be multiple of M perhaps or not) whose sum = M.
> 
> We can apply same method as for c>0 but with d = 0? Actually c=0 gives N = M (no extra part). In that case we need three distinct divisors of L summing to M. Since sum is exactly
